<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Defamation</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="hatnote" role="note">For the film, see <a href="/wiki/Defamation_(film)" title="Defamation (film)">Defamation (film)</a>.</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">"Libel" and "Slander" redirect here. For Wikipedia's policy, see <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Libel" title="Wikipedia:Libel">Wikipedia:Libel</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Libel_(disambiguation)" title="Libel (disambiguation)">Libel (disambiguation)</a> and <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Slander_(disambiguation)" title="Slander (disambiguation)">Slander (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p><b>Defamation</b>—also <b>calumny</b>, <b>vilification</b>, and <b>traducement</b>—is the communication of a <a href="/wiki/False_accusation" title="False accusation">false statement</a> that harms the reputation of an <a href="/wiki/Individual" title="Individual">individual</a> person, <a href="/wiki/Business" title="Business">business</a>, <a href="/wiki/Product_(business)" title="Product (business)">product</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_group" title="Social group">group</a>, <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">government</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Nation" title="Nation">nation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Under <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a>, to constitute defamation, a claim must generally be false and must have been made to someone other than the person defamed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> jurisdictions also distinguish between spoken defamation, called <b>slander</b>, and defamation in other media such as printed words or images, called <b>libel</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/False_light" title="False light">False light</a> laws protect against statements which are not technically false, but which are misleading.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-martin_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-martin-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>In some <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law</a> jurisdictions, defamation is treated as a <a href="/wiki/Crime" title="Crime">crime</a> rather than a <a href="/wiki/Civil_wrong" title="Civil wrong">civil wrong</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Human_Rights_Committee" title="United Nations Human Rights Committee">United Nations Human Rights Committee</a> ruled in 2012 that the libel law of one country, the Philippines, was inconsistent with Article 19 of the <a href="/wiki/International_Covenant_on_Civil_and_Political_Rights" title="International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights">International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights</a>, as well as urging that "State parties [to the Covenant] should consider the decriminalization of libel".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2012UNHCRlibel_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-2012UNHCRlibel-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>A person who defames another may be called a "defamer", "libeler", "slanderer", or, rarely, "famacide".</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Slander">Slander</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Slander">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> origins of defamation lie in the <a href="/wiki/Tort" title="Tort">torts</a> of "slander" (harmful statement in a transient form, especially speech), each of which gives a common law right of action.</p>
<p><i>Defamation</i> is the general term used internationally, and is used in this article where it is not necessary to distinguish between "slander" and "libel". Libel and slander both require publication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The fundamental distinction between libel and slander lies solely in the <i>form</i> in which the defamatory matter is published. If the offending material is published in some fleeting form, as by spoken words or sounds, sign language, gestures or the like, then it is slander.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Libel">Libel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Libel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Libel is defined as defamation by written or printed words, pictures, or in any form other than by spoken words or gestures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The law of libel originated in the 17th century in England. With the growth of publication came the growth of libel and development of the <a href="/wiki/Tort" title="Tort">tort</a> of libel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cases_involving_libel">Cases involving libel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Cases involving libel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>An early example of libel is the case of <a href="/wiki/John_Peter_Zenger" title="John Peter Zenger">John Peter Zenger</a> in 1735. Zenger was hired to publish <i>New York Weekly Journal</i>. When he printed another man's article that criticized <a href="/wiki/William_Cosby" title="William Cosby">William Cosby</a>, who was then British <a href="/wiki/Colonial_government_in_the_Thirteen_Colonies" title="Colonial government in the Thirteen Colonies">Royal Governor</a> of <a href="/wiki/Province_of_New_York" title="Province of New York">Colonial New York</a>, Zenger was accused of <a href="/wiki/Seditious_libel" title="Seditious libel">Seditious Libel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The verdict was returned as <i>Not Guilty</i> on the charge of seditious libel, because it was proven that all the statements Zenger had published about Cosby had been true, so there was not an issue of defamation. Another example of libel is the case of <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Times_Co._v._Sullivan" title="New York Times Co. v. Sullivan">New York Times Co. v. Sullivan</a></i> (1964). The <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">U.S. Supreme Court</a> overruled a State court in <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a> that had found <i>The New York Times</i> guilty of libel for printing an advertisement that criticized Alabama officials for mistreating student <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_rights" title="Civil rights">civil rights</a> activists. Even though some of what <i>The Times</i> printed was false, the Court ruled in its favor, saying that libel of a public official requires proof of <a href="/wiki/Actual_malice" title="Actual malice">actual malice</a>, which was defined as a "knowing or reckless disregard for the truth".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Proving_libel">Proving libel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Proving libel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There are several ways a person must go about proving that libel has taken place. For example, in the United States, the person must prove that the statement was false, caused harm, and was made without adequate research into the truthfulness of the statement. These steps are for an ordinary citizen. For a celebrity or a public official, the person must prove the first three steps <i>and</i> that the statement was made with the intent to do harm or with reckless disregard for the truth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> which is usually specifically referred to as "proving malice".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criminal_defamation">Criminal defamation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Criminal defamation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Defamatory_libel" title="Defamatory libel">Defamatory libel</a></div>
<p>Many nations have criminal penalties for defamation in some situations, and different conditions for determining whether an offense has occurred. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ARTICLE_19" title="ARTICLE 19">ARTICLE 19</a>, a free expression advocacy group, has published global maps<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> charting the existence of criminal defamation law across the globe, as well as showing countries that have special protections for political leaders or functionaries of the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>There can be regional statutes that may differ from the national norm. For example, in the United States, defamation is generally limited to the living. However, there are nine states (Idaho, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kansas" title="Kansas">Kansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a>) that have criminal statutes regarding defamation of the dead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Organization_for_Security_and_Co-operation_in_Europe" title="Organization for Security and Co-operation in Europe">Organization for Security and Co-operation in Europe</a> (OSCE) has also published a detailed database on criminal and civil defamation provisions in 55 countries, including all European countries, all member countries of the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Independent_States" title="Commonwealth of Independent States">Commonwealth of Independent States</a>, the United States and Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a 2012 ruling on a complaint filed by a broadcaster who had been imprisoned for violating Philippine libel law, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Commission_on_Human_Rights" title="United Nations Commission on Human Rights">United Nations Commission on Human Rights</a> held that the criminalization of libel without provision of a public figure doctrine – as in Philippine criminal law – violates <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freedom_of_expression" title="Freedom of expression">freedom of expression</a> and is inconsistent with Article 19 of the <a href="/wiki/International_Covenant_on_Civil_and_Political_Rights" title="International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights">International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2012UNHCRlibel_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-2012UNHCRlibel-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>Early cases of criminal defamation</b></p>
<p>Questions of group libel have been appearing in common law for hundreds of years. One of the earliest known cases of a defendant being tried for defamation of a group was the case of Rex v. Orme and Nutt (1700). In this case, the jury found that the defendant was guilty of libeling several subjects, though they did not specifically identify who these subjects were. A report of the case told that the jury believed that “where a writing … inveighs against mankind in general, or against a particular order of men, as for instance, men of the gown, this is no libel, but it must descend to particulars and individuals to make it libel.” <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> This jury believed that only individuals who believed they were specifically defamed had a claim to a libel case. Since the jury was unable to identify the exact people who were being defamed, there was no cause to identify the statements were a libel.</p>
<p>Another early English group libel which has been frequently cited is King v. Osborne (1732). In this case, the defendant was on trial “for printing a libel reflecting upon the Portuguese Jews.” The printing in question claimed that Jews who had arrived in London from Portugal burned a Jewish woman to death when she had a child with a Christian man, and that this act was common. Following Osborne’s anti-Semitic publication, several Jews were attacked. Initially, the judge seemed to believe the court could do nothing since no individual was singled out by Osborne’s writings. However, the court concluded that “since the publication implied the act was one Jews frequently did, the whole community of Jews was defamed.” <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Though various reports of this case give differing accounts of the crime, this report clearly shows a ruling based on group libel. Since laws restricting libel were accepted at this time because of its tendency to lead to a breach of peace, group libel laws were justified because they showed potential for an equal or perhaps greater risk of violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-20">[20]</a></sup> For this reason, group libel cases are criminal even though most libel cases are civil torts.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>From early times, people have comprehended defamatory and injurious statements made in a public manner (<i>convicium adversus bonos mores</i>).</p>
<p>The <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Praetorian_Edict&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Praetorian Edict (page does not exist)">Praetorian Edict</a>, codified circa 130 A.D., declared that an action could be brought up for shouting at someone contrary to good morals: "qui, adversus bonos mores convicium cui fecisse cuiusve opera factum esse dicitur, quo adversus bonos mores convicium fieret, in eum iudicium dabo."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> In this case the essence of the offense lay in the unwarrantable public proclamation. According to <a href="/wiki/Ulpian" title="Ulpian">Ulpian</a>, not all shouting was actionable. Drawing on the argument of <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Antistius_Labeo" title="Marcus Antistius Labeo">Labeo</a>, he asserted that the offense consisted in shouting contrary to the morals of the city ("adversus bonos mores huius civitatis") something apt to bring in disrepute or contempt ("quae... ad infamiam vel invidiam alicuius spectaret") the person exposed thereto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Any act apt to bring another person into disrepute gave rise to an <i>actio injurarum</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> In such a case the truth of the statements was no justification for the public and insulting manner in which they had been made. But even in public matters, the accused had the opportunity to justify his actions by openly stating what he considered necessary for public safety to be denounced by the libel, and proving his assertions to be true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The second head included defamatory statements made in private, and in this case the offense lay in the content of the imputation, not in the manner of its publication. The truth was therefore a sufficient defense, for no man had a right to demand legal protection for a false reputation.</p>
<p>Roman law was aimed at giving sufficient scope for the discussion of a man's character, while it protected him from needless insult and pain. The remedy for verbal defamation was long confined to a civil action for a monetary penalty, which was estimated according to the significance of the case, and which, although vindictive in its character, doubtless included practically the element of compensation. But a new remedy was introduced with the extension of the criminal law, under which many kinds of defamation were punished with great severity. At the same time increased importance attached to the publication of defamatory books and writings, the <i>libri</i> or <i>libelli famosi</i>, from which we derive our modern use of the word libel; and under the later emperors the latter term came to be specially applied to anonymous accusations or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pasquil" title="Pasquil">pasquils</a>, the dissemination of which was regarded as particularly dangerous, and visited with very severe punishment, whether the matter contained in them were true or false.</p>
<p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_England" title="Anglo-Saxon England">Anglo-Saxon England</a>, slander was punished by cutting out the <a href="/wiki/Tongue" title="Tongue">tongue</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Defenses">Defenses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Defenses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Even if a statement is defamatory, there are circumstances in which such statements are permissible in law.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Truth">Truth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Truth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Substantial_truth" title="Substantial truth">Substantial truth</a></div>
<p>In many legal systems, adverse public statements about legal citizens presented as fact must be proven false to be defamatory or slanderous/libellous.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Proving adverse public character statements to be true is often the best defense against a prosecution for libel or defamation. Statements of opinion that cannot be proven true or false will likely need to apply some other kind of defense. The use of the defense of justification has dangers, however; if the defendant libels the plaintiff and then runs the defense of truth and fails, he may be said to have aggravated the harm.</p>
<p>Another important aspect of defamation is the difference between fact and opinion. Statements made as "facts" are frequently actionable defamation. Statements of opinion or pure opinion are not actionable. Some jurisdictions decline to recognize any legal distinction between fact and opinion. To win damages in a libel case, the plaintiff must first show that the statements were "statements of fact or mixed statements of opinion and fact" and second that these statements were false. Conversely, a typical defense to defamation is that the statements are opinion, relying on <a href="/wiki/Opinion_privilege" title="Opinion privilege">opinion privilege</a>. One of the major tests to distinguish whether a statement is fact or opinion is whether the statement can be proved true or false in a court of law. If the statement can be proved true or false, then, on that basis, the case will be heard by a jury to determine whether it is true or false. If the statement cannot be proved true or false, the court may dismiss the libel case without it ever going to a jury to find facts in the case.</p>
<p>Under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_common_law" title="English common law">English common law</a>, proving the truth of the allegation was originally a valid defense only in civil libel cases. Criminal libel was construed as an offence against the public at large based on the tendency of the libel to provoke <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breach_of_peace" title="Breach of peace">breach of peace</a>, rather than being a crime based upon the actual defamation <i>per se</i>; its veracity was therefore considered irrelevant. Section 6 of the <a href="/wiki/Libel_Act_1843" title="Libel Act 1843">Libel Act 1843</a> allowed the proven truth of the allegation to be used as a valid defense in criminal libel cases, but only if the defendant also demonstrated that publication was for the "Public Benefit".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>In some systems, however, notably the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, truth alone is not a defense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPdefamation_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-RPdefamation-27">[27]</a></sup> Some U.S. statutes preserve historical common law exceptions to the defense of truth to libel actions. These exceptions were for statements "tending to blacken the memory of one who is dead" or "<a href="/wiki/Privacy_law" title="Privacy law">expose the natural defects</a> of one who is alive".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>It is also necessary in these cases to show that there is a well-founded <a href="/wiki/Public_interest" title="Public interest">public interest</a> in the specific information being widely known, and this may be the case even for <a href="/wiki/Public_figure" title="Public figure">public figures</a>. Public interest is generally not "what the public is interested in", but rather "what is in the interest of the public".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Noonan v. Staples</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> is sometimes cited as precedent that truth is not always a defense to libel in the U.S., but the case is actually not valid precedent on that issue because Staples did not argue First Amendment protection, which is one theory for truth as complete defense, for its statements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The court assumed in this case that the Massachusetts law was constitutional under the First Amendment without it being argued by the parties.</p>
<p>In a 2012 ruling involving Philippine libel law, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Commission_on_Human_Rights" title="United Nations Commission on Human Rights">United Nations Commission on Human Rights</a> commented, "Penal defamation laws should include defense of truth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2012UNHCRlibel_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-2012UNHCRlibel-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Privilege_and_malice">Privilege and malice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Privilege and malice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Privilege provides a complete bar and answer to a defamation suit, though conditions may have to be met before this protection is granted. Privilege is any circumstance that justifies or excuses a prima facie tort. It can be said that privilege recognizes a defendant's action stemmed from an interest of social importance – and that society wants to protect such interests by not punishing those who pursue them. Privilege can be argued whenever a defendant can show that he acted from a justifiable motive. While some privileges have long been recognized, the court may create a new privilege for particular circumstances – privilege as an affirmative defense is a potentially ever-evolving doctrine. Such newly created or circumstantially recognized privileges are referred to as residual justification privileges.</p>
<p>There are two types of privilege in the common law tradition:</p>
<ul>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_privilege" title="Parliamentary privilege">Absolute privilege</a>" has the effect that a statement cannot be sued on as defamatory, even if it were made maliciously; a typical example is evidence given in court (although this may give rise to different claims, such as an action for malicious prosecution or perjury) or statements made in a session of the legislature (known as 'Parliamentary privilege' in Commonwealth countries).</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Qualified_privilege" title="Qualified privilege">Qualified privilege</a>" may be available to the journalist as a defense in circumstances where it is considered important that the facts be known in the public interest; an example would be public meetings, local government documents, and information relating to public bodies such as the police and fire departments. Another example would be that a professor – acting in good faith and honesty – may write an unsatisfactory letter of reference with unsatisfactory information. Qualified privilege has the same effect as absolute privilege, but does not protect statements that can be proven to have been made with malicious intent.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_defenses">Other defenses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Other defenses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Defenses to claims of defamation include:</p>
<ul>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mistake_of_fact" title="Mistake of fact">Statements made in a good faith and reasonable belief that they were true</a></b> are generally treated the same as true statements; however, the court may inquire into the reasonableness of the belief. The degree of care expected will vary with the nature of the defendant: an ordinary person might safely rely on a single newspaper report, while the newspaper would be expected to carefully check multiple sources.</li>
<li><b>Opinion</b> is a defense recognized in nearly every jurisdiction. If the allegedly defamatory assertion is an expression of opinion rather than a statement of fact, defamation claims usually cannot be brought because opinions are inherently not <a href="/wiki/Falsifiability" title="Falsifiability">falsifiable</a>. However, some jurisdictions decline to recognize any legal distinction between fact and opinion. The United States Supreme Court, in particular, has ruled that the First Amendment does not require recognition of an <a href="/wiki/Opinion_privilege" title="Opinion privilege">opinion privilege</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mere_vulgar_abuse&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mere vulgar abuse (page does not exist)">Mere vulgar abuse</a></b> is an insult that is not necessarily defamatory because it is not intended to be taken literally or believed, or likely to cause real damage to a reputation. Vituperative statements made in anger, such as calling someone "an arse" during a drunken argument, would likely be considered mere vulgar abuse and not defamatory.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Fair_comment" title="Fair comment">Fair comment on a matter of public interest</a></b>, arguments made with an honest belief in their soundness on a matter of public interest (such as regarding official acts) are defendable against a defamation claim, even if such arguments are <a href="/wiki/Soundness" title="Soundness">logically unsound</a>; if a <a href="/wiki/Reasonable_person" title="Reasonable person">reasonable person</a> could honestly entertain such an opinion, the statement is protected. In the US fair comment is a common law defense, and it has been argued has been superseded by constitutional defences.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Consent" title="Consent">Consent</a></b> is an uncommon defense and makes the claim that the <a href="/wiki/Petitioner" title="Petitioner">claimant</a> consented to the dissemination of the statement.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Innocent_dissemination" title="Innocent dissemination">Innocent dissemination</a></b> is a defense available when a <a href="/wiki/Defendant" title="Defendant">defendant</a> had no actual knowledge of the defamatory statement or no reason to believe the statement was defamatory. Thus, a delivery service cannot be held liable for delivering a sealed defamatory letter. The defense can be defeated if the lack of knowledge was due to <a href="/wiki/Negligence" title="Negligence">negligence</a>.</li>
<li>Claimant is <b>incapable of further defamation</b> – e.g., the claimant's position in the community is so poor that defamation could not do further damage to the plaintiff. Such a claimant could be said to be "libel-proof", since in most jurisdictions, actual damage is an essential element for a libel claim. Essentially, the defense is that the person had such a bad <a href="/wiki/Reputation" title="Reputation">reputation</a> before the libel, that no further <a href="/wiki/Damages" title="Damages">damage</a> could possibly have been caused by the making of the statement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Statute_of_limitations" title="Statute of limitations">Statute of limitations</a>. Most jurisdictions require that a lawsuit be brought within a limited period of time. If the alleged libel occurs in a mass media publication such as a newspaper or the Internet, the statute of limitations begins to run at the time of publication, not when the plaintiff first learns of the communication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>No Third-party communication</b>: If an employer were to bring an employee into a sound-proof, isolated room, and accuse him of embezzling company money, the employee would have no defamation recourse, since no one other than the would-be plaintiff and would-be defendant heard the false statement.</li>
<li><b>No actual injury</b>: If there <i>is</i> third-party communication, but the third-party hearing the defamatory statement does not believe the statement, or does not care, then there is no injury, and therefore, no recourse.</li>
<li><b>Slander per-se:</b> is an exception to Slander (presume general damages). Slander per-se states that an individual has: 1. A loathsome disease, 2. Business improprieties, 3. Committed a crime or have been in prison for a crime, 4. Committed sexual improprieties/impotent.</li>
</ul>
<p>In addition to the above, the defendant may claim that the allegedly defamatory statement is not actually capable of being defamatory—an insulting statement that does not actually harm someone's reputation is <i><a href="/wiki/Prima_facie" title="Prima facie">prima facie</a></i> not libelous. Also, the <b>public figure</b> doctrine, also called the absence of malice rule, may be used as a defense.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_figure_doctrine_.28absence_of_malice.29">Public figure doctrine (absence of malice)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Public figure doctrine (absence of malice)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the United States, special rules apply in the case of statements made in the press concerning public figures, which can be used as a defense. A series of court rulings led by <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Times_Co._v._Sullivan" title="New York Times Co. v. Sullivan">New York Times Co. v. Sullivan</a></i>, 376 U.S. 254 (1964) established that for a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_official" title="Public official">public official</a> (or other legitimate public figure) to win a libel case in the United States, the statement must have been published knowing it to be false or with reckless disregard to its truth (also known as <a href="/wiki/Actual_malice" title="Actual malice">actual malice</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Under United States law, libel generally requires five key elements: the plaintiff must prove that the information was published, the plaintiff was directly or indirectly identified, the remarks were defamatory towards the plaintiff's reputation, the published information is false, and that the defendant is at fault.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a> estimates that 95% of libel cases involving news stories do not arise from high-profile news stories, but "run of the mill" local stories like news coverage of local criminal investigations or trials, or business profiles.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Media liability insurance is available to newspapers to cover potential damage awards from libel lawsuits.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Freedom_of_speech">Freedom of speech</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Freedom of speech">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Defamation laws may come into tension with <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_speech" title="Freedom of speech">freedom of speech</a>, leading to censorship or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chilling_effect_(term)" title="Chilling effect (term)">chilling effects</a> where publishers fear lawsuits. Article 10 of the European Convention on Human Rights permits restrictions on freedom of speech when necessary to protect the reputation or rights of others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jurisdictions resolve this tension in different ways, in particular in determining where the burden of proof lies when unfounded allegations are made. The power of the internet to disseminate comment, which may include malicious comment, has brought a new focus to the issue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is a broader consensus against laws that <i>criminalize</i> defamation. Human rights organizations, and other organizations such as the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Organization_for_Security_and_Co-operation_in_Europe" title="Organization for Security and Co-operation in Europe">Organization for Security and Co-operation in Europe</a>, have campaigned against strict defamation laws that criminalize defamation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The European Court of Human Rights has placed restrictions on criminal libel laws because of the freedom of expression provisions of the European Convention on Human Rights. One notable case was <i><a href="/wiki/Lingens_v._Austria" title="Lingens v. Austria">Lingens v. Austria</a></i> (1986).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Laws_by_jurisdiction">Laws by jurisdiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Laws by jurisdiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internationally">Internationally</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Internationally">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Article 17 of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> <a href="/wiki/International_Covenant_on_Civil_and_Political_Rights" title="International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights">International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights</a> states</p>
<dl>
<dd>1. No one shall be subjected to arbitrary or unlawful interference with his privacy, family, home or correspondence, nor to unlawful attacks on his honour and reputation.</dd>
<dd>2. Everyone has the right to the protection of the law against such interference or attacks.</dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Azerbaijan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>, the crime of defamation (Article 147) may result in a fine up to "500 times the amount of <a href="/wiki/List_of_minimum_wages_by_country" title="List of minimum wages by country">minimum salaries</a>", public work for up to 240 hours, correctional work for up to one year, or imprisonment of up to six months. Penalties are aggravated to up to three years of prison if the victim is falsely accused of having committed a crime "of grave or very grave nature" (Article 147.2). The crime of insult (Article 148) can lead to a fine of up to 1000 times the minimum wage, or to the same penalties of defamation for public work, correctional work or imprisonment. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the OSCE report on defamation laws, "Azerbaijan intends to remove articles on defamation and insult from criminal legislation and preserve them in the Civil Code".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Article 246 of the Criminal Law of the People's Republic of China (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/zh:%E4%B8%AD%E5%8D%8E%E4%BA%BA%E6%B0%91%E5%85%B1%E5%92%8C%E5%9B%BD%E5%88%91%E6%B3%95" title="s:zh:中华人民共和国刑法">中华人民共和国刑法</a>) criminalizes defamation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Defamation can be prosecuted either criminally or civilly, according to what is followed under Article 230-1 of the Criminal Code of Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_India" title="Constitution of India">Constitution of India</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fundamental_Rights_in_India#Right_to_freedom" title="Fundamental Rights in India">fundamental right to free speech (Article 19)</a> is subject to <b>reasonable restrictions</b>. Accordingly, for the purpose of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Criminal" title="Criminal">criminal</a> defamation, <i>"reasonable restrictions"</i> are defined in <i>Section 499</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> of <a href="/wiki/Indian_Penal_Code" title="Indian Penal Code">Indian Penal Code, 1860</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Defamation_litigation:_a_survivor.27s_kit_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Defamation_litigation:_a_survivor.27s_kit-47">[47]</a></sup> This section defines Defamation and provides valid exceptions when a statement is not considered to be Defamation. It says that Defamation takes place "by words either spoken or intended to be read, or by signs or by visible representations, to make or publish any imputation concerning any person intending to harm, or knowing or having reason to believe that such imputation will harm, the reputation, of such person."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPC_.40_Bombay_High_Court_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPC_.40_Bombay_High_Court-48">[48]</a></sup> In India, a defamation case can be filed under either <a href="/wiki/Criminal_law" title="Criminal law">criminal law</a> or <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(common_law)" title="Civil law (common law)">civil law</a> or Cyber Crime Law, together or in sequence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Defamation_litigation:_a_survivor.27s_kit_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Defamation_litigation:_a_survivor.27s_kit-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>The punishment for Defamation is a simple imprisonment for up to two years or with fine or with both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPC_.40_Bombay_High_Court_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-IPC_.40_Bombay_High_Court-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPC_.40_punjabrevenue.nic.in_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPC_.40_punjabrevenue.nic.in-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Korea">South Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: South Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cyber_defamation_law#South_Korea" title="Cyber defamation law">Cyber defamation law § South Korea</a></div>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-Tone" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section 's <b>tone or style may not reflect the <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Tone" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles">encyclopedic tone</a> used on Wikipedia</b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">See Wikipedia's <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Tone" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles">guide to writing better articles</a> for suggestions.</span> <small><i>(March 2013)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>In Korea true and false statements are punishable criminally and civilly with defamation; any words harming another can be considered illegal and may be punishable with fines and imprisonment up to seven years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Defamation is covered by several laws in Korea, civil law, traditional criminal law and modern internet criminal law-under the "Act on Promotion of Information and Communications Network Utilization and Data Protection, etc." (Internet and email related laws) – 2005 CHAPTER IX Article 61 (Penal Provisions). Korean defamation varies significantly from Western laws and in general by country and by case. As image and "public <a href="/wiki/Face_(sociological_concept)" title="Face (sociological concept)">face</a>" are very important in East Asia, it is not difficult to sue for "loss of face" (defamation) in Korea. Even middle school students are active with cases.</p>
<ol>
<li>Any person who has defamed any other person by alleging openly facts through information and communications networks [anyone stating true comments by internet and email] with the purpose of slandering him shall be subject to imprisonment with or without prison labor for not more than 3 years or by a fine not exceeding 20 million won.</li>
<li>Any person who has defamed any other person by alleging openly false facts via information and communications networks [anyone stating false comments on the internet and email] with the purpose of slandering him/her shall be subject to imprisonment with prison labor for not more than 7 years or the suspension of disqualification for not more than 10 years, or by a fine not exceeding 50 million won [approximately US$50,000. In addition to this criminal law, in Korea you can also sue for damages with civil actions. Generally, criminal actions proceed civil ones with Korean police as judicial investigators.].</li>
</ol>
<p>As of June 2010, Korean courts were still hearing cases and individuals frequently fined a few thousand dollars for true facts. International "comity" procedure or "intent" appear not key in Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Former_Soviet_Union">Former Soviet Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Former Soviet Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the former <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, defamatory insults "could only constitute a criminal offense, not a civil wrong".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nepal">Nepal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Nepal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Defamation Act, 1859</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Philippines">Philippines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Philippines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Title thirteen of the <a href="/wiki/Revised_Penal_Code_of_the_Philippines" title="Revised Penal Code of the Philippines">Revised Penal Code of the Philippines</a> addresses <i>Crimes Against Honor</i>. Chapter one of that title addresses libel and slander. Libel is defined as "public and malicious imputation of a crime, or of a vice or defect, real or imaginary, or any act, omission, condition, status, or circumstance tending to cause the dishonor, discredit, or contempt of a natural or juridical person, or to blacken the memory of one who is dead." Slander is defined as oral defamation. Slander by deed is defined as "any act not included and punished in this title, which shall cast dishonor, discredit or contempt upon another person." Penalties of fine or imprisonment are specified for these crimes and for the threat of libel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPdefamation_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-RPdefamation-27">[27]</a></sup> A notable characteristic of these crimes under Philippine law is the specification that they apply to imputations both real and imaginary.</p>
<p>In 2012, the Philippines enacted Republic Act 10175, titled <i><a href="/wiki/Cybercrime_Prevention_Act_of_2012" title="Cybercrime Prevention Act of 2012">The Cybercrime Prevention Act of 2012</a></i>. Essentially, this Act provides that libel is criminally punishable and describes it as: "Libel – the unlawful or prohibited act as defined in Article 355 of the Revised Penal Code, as amended, committed through a computer system or any other similar means which may be devised in the future." Professor Harry Roque of the University of the Philippines has written that under this law, electronic libel is punished with imprisonment from 6 years and one day to up to 12 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> As of 30 September 2012<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, five petitions claiming the law to be unconstitutional had been filed with the Philippine Supreme Court, one by Senator <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teofisto_Guingona_III" title="Teofisto Guingona III">Teofisto Guingona III</a>. The petitions all claim that the law infringes on freedom of expression, due process, equal protection and privacy of communication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Taiwan">Taiwan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Taiwan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Article 310 of the Criminal Code of the Republic of China (<a class="extiw" href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E4%B8%AD%E8%8F%AF%E6%B0%91%E5%9C%8B%E5%88%91%E6%B3%95" title="zh:中華民國刑法">中華民國刑法</a>) criminalizes defamation, held constitutional on 7 July 2000 by the Justices of the Constitutional Court, Judicial Yuan (<a class="extiw" href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E5%8F%B8%E6%B3%95%E9%99%A2%E5%A4%A7%E6%B3%95%E5%AE%98" title="zh:司法院大法官">司法院大法官</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Thailand">Thailand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Thailand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil">Civil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Civil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>"A person who, contrary to the truth, asserts or circulates as a fact that which injurious to the reputation or the credit of another or his earnings or prosperity in any other manner, shall compensate the other for any damage arising therefrom, even if he does not know of its untruth, provided he ought to know it.</p>
<p>A person who makes a communication the untruth of which is unknown to him, does not thereby render himself liable to make compensation, if he or the receiver of the communication has a rightful interest in it.</p>
<p>The Court, when given judgment as to the liability for wrongful act and the amount of compensation, shall not be bound by the provisions of the criminal law concerning liability to punishment or by the conviction or non-conviction of the wrongdoer for a criminal offence."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Criminal">Criminal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Criminal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>"Section 326. Defamation</p>
<p>Whoever, imputes anything to the other person before a third person in a manner likely to impair the reputation of such other person or to expose such other person to be hated or scorned, is said to commit defamation, and shall be punished with imprisonment not exceeding one year or fined not exceeding twenty thousand Baht, or both. Section 327. Defamation to the Family</p>
<p>Whoever, imputing anything the deceased person before the third person, and that imputation to be likely to impair the reputation of the father, mother, spouse or child of the deceased or to expose that person hated or scammed to be said to commit defamation, and shall be punished as prescribed by Section 326."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Albania">Albania</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Albania">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>According to the Criminal Code of <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a>, defamation is a crime. Insulting (Article 119) can lead to a fine or up to six months of imprisonment (if in public, up to a year), while libel (Article 120) may result in a fine or up to a year of prison (up to 2 years when in public). In addition, defamation of authorities, public officials or foreign representatives (Articles 227, 239 to 241) are separate crimes with maximum penalties varying from 1 to 3 years of imprisonment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Austria">Austria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Austria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Austria, the crime of defamation is foreseen by Article 111 of the Criminal Code. Related criminal offenses include "slander and assault" (Article 115), that happens "if a person insults, mocks, mistreats or threatens will ill-treatment another one in public", and yet "malicious falsehood" (Article 297), defined as a false accusation that exposes someone to the risk of prosecution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Belgium">Belgium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Belgium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Belgium, crimes against honour are foreseen in Chapter V of the Belgian Penal Code, Articles 443 to 453-bis. Someone is guilty of calumny « when law admits <a href="/wiki/Evidence_(law)" title="Evidence (law)">proof</a> of the alleged fact » and of defamation "when law does not admit this evidence" (Article 443). The penalty is 8 days to one year of imprisonment, plus a fine (Article 444). In addition, the crime of "calumnious denunciation" (Article 445) is punished with 15 days to six months in prison, plus a fine. In any of the crimes covered by Chapter V of the Penal Code, the minimum penalty may be doubled (Article 453-bis) « when one of the motivations of the crime is hatred, contempt or hostility of a person due to his or her <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Race_(classification_of_human_beings)" title="Race (classification of human beings)">intended race</a>, color of the skin, <a href="/wiki/Ancestor" title="Ancestor">ancestry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nationality" title="Nationality">national origin</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_group" title="Ethnic group">ethnicity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nationality" title="Nationality">nationality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gender" title="Gender">gender</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sexual_orientation" title="Sexual orientation">sexual orientation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marital_status" title="Marital status">marital status</a>, place of birth, age, <a href="/wiki/Property" title="Property">patrimony</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">philosophical</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_belief" title="Religious belief">religious belief</a>, present or future health condition, <a href="/wiki/Disability" title="Disability">disability</a>, <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">native language</a>, political belief, physical or genetical characteristic, or <a href="/wiki/Social_class" title="Social class">social origin</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Bulgaria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, defamation is formally a criminal offense, but the penalty of imprisonment has been abolished in 1999. Articles 146 (insult), 147 (criminal defamation) and 148 (public insult) of the Criminal Code prescribe a penalty of fine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Croatia">Croatia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Croatia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a>, the crime of insult prescribes a penalty of up to three months in prison, or a fine of "up to 100 daily incomes" (Criminal Code, Article 199). If the crime is committed in public, penalties are aggravated to up to six months of imprisonment, or a fine of "up to 150 daily incomes" (Article 199-2). Moreover, the crime of defamation occurs when someone affirms or disseminates false facts about other person that can damage his reputation. The maximum penalty is one year in prison, or a fine of up to 150 daily incomes (Article 200-1). If the crime is committed in public, the prison term can reach one year (Article 200-2). On the other hand, according to Article 203, there is an exemption for the application of the aforementioned articles (insult and defamation) when the <a href="/wiki/Context_(language_use)" title="Context (language use)">specific context</a> is that of a <a href="/wiki/Scientific_literature" title="Scientific literature">scientific work</a>, <a href="/wiki/Literature" title="Literature">literary work</a>, <a href="/wiki/Work_of_art" title="Work of art">work of art</a>, public information conducted by a politician or a government official, <a href="/wiki/Journalism" title="Journalism">journalistic work</a>, or the defense of a right or the protection of justifiable interests, in all cases <i>provided that</i> the conduct was not aimed at damaging someone's reputation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Czech_Republic">Czech Republic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Czech Republic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>According to the Czech Criminal Code, Article 184, defamation is a crime. Penalties may reach a maximum prison term of one year (Article 184-1) or, if the crime is committed through the press, film, radio, TV, publicly accessible computer network, or by "similarly effective" methods, the offender may stay in prison for up to two years or be prohibited of exercising a specific activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> However, only the most severe cases will be subjets to criminal liability. The less severe cases can be solved by an action for apology, damages or injunctions.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Denmark">Denmark</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Denmark">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Denmark, libel is a crime, as defined by Article 267 of the Danish Criminal Code, with a penalty of up to six months in prison or a fine, with proceedings initiated by the victim. In addition, Article 266-b prescribes a maximum prison term of two years in the case of public defamation aimed at a group of persons because of their race, color, national or ethnic origin, religion or "sexual inclination".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Finland">Finland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Finland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Finland, defamation is a crime, according to the <a href="/wiki/Criminal_Code_of_Finland" title="Criminal Code of Finland">Criminal Code</a> (Chapter 24, Section 9), with a penalty of imprisonment of up to six months or a fine. When the defamation occurs in public, the crime is "aggravated defamation" (Chapter 24, Section 10), with a maximum punishment of two years in prison or a fine. In addition, there's also a crime called "dissemination of information violating personal privacy" (Chapter 24, Section 8), that deals with the public dissemination of information that can harm one's private life. However, personalities involved in the fields of politics, business, public office or public position, "or in a comparable position", are specifically not protected by this article.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CdG_LA_VIE_ILLUSTREE_1902_N_197_L%27AFFAIRE_Mme_DUGAST-_Me_BARBOUX_25_July_1902.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="225" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/CdG_LA_VIE_ILLUSTREE_1902_N_197_L%27AFFAIRE_Mme_DUGAST-_Me_BARBOUX_25_July_1902.JPG/220px-CdG_LA_VIE_ILLUSTREE_1902_N_197_L%27AFFAIRE_Mme_DUGAST-_Me_BARBOUX_25_July_1902.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/00/CdG_LA_VIE_ILLUSTREE_1902_N_197_L%27AFFAIRE_Mme_DUGAST-_Me_BARBOUX_25_July_1902.JPG 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CdG_LA_VIE_ILLUSTREE_1902_N_197_L%27AFFAIRE_Mme_DUGAST-_Me_BARBOUX_25_July_1902.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Front page of <i>La Vie Illustrée</i> on 25 July 1902. <a href="/wiki/Camille_du_Gast" title="Camille du Gast">Mme Camille du Gast</a> stands in court during the cases of <a href="/wiki/Domino_mask#La_Femme_au_Masque" title="Domino mask">character defamation</a> by the barrister Maître Barboux, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H%C3%A9lie_de_Talleyrand-P%C3%A9rigord,_Duc_de_Sagan" title="Hélie de Talleyrand-Périgord, Duc de Sagan">Prince of Sagan</a>'s assault on Barboux.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In France, defamation is a criminal offense defined as "the allegation or [the] allocation of a fact that damages the honor or reputation of the person or body to which the fact is imputed". A defamatory allegation is considered an insult if it doesn't include any facts or if the claimed facts can't be verified.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany">Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In German law, there is no distinction between libel and slander. German defamation lawsuits are increasing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The relevant offences of Germany's <a href="/wiki/Strafgesetzbuch#German_StGB" title="Strafgesetzbuch">Criminal Code</a> are §90 (Denigration of the President of State), §90a (Denigration of the State and its Symbols), §90b (Unconstitutional denigration of the Organs of the Constitution), §185 ("insult"), §186 (Defamation of character), §187 (Defamation with deliberate untruths), §188 (Political defamation with increased penalties for offending against paras 186 and 187), §189 (Denigration of a deceased person), §192 ("insult" with true statements). Other sections relevant to prosecution of these offences are §190 (Criminal conviction as proof of truth), §193 (No defamation in the pursuit of rightful interests), §194 (The Application for a criminal prosecution under these paragraphs), §199 (Mutual insult allowed to be left unpunished), and §200 (Method of proclamation). Paragraph 188 has been criticized<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (September 2009)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> for allowing certain public figures additional protection against criticism.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Greece">Greece</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Greece">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Greece, the maximum prison term for defamation, libel or insult is five years, while the maximum fine is €15,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>The crime of insult (Article 361, § 1, of the Penal Code) may lead to up to one year of imprisonment and/or a fine, while unprovoked insult (Article 361-A, § 1) is punished with at least three months in prison. In addition, defamation may result in up to two months in prison and/or a fine, while aggravated defamation can lead to at least 3 months of prison, plus a possible fine (Article 363) and deprivation of the offender's <a href="/wiki/Civil_and_political_rights" title="Civil and political rights">civil rights</a>. Finally, disparaging the memory of a deceased person is punished with imprisonment of up to 6 months (Penal Code, Article 365). <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ireland">Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Individuals are protected under the Defamation Act 2009 which came into force on the first of January 2010. This 2009 Act repeals the Defamation Act 1961, which had, together with the underlying principles of the common law of tort, governed Irish defamation law for almost half a century. The 2009 Act represents significant changes in Irish law, as many believe that it previously attached insufficient importance to the media's freedom of expression and weighed too heavily in favour of the individual's right to a good name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The Act has a one-year limitation period which can be extended to two years in exceptional circumstances.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Italy">Italy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Italy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Italy, there used to be different crimes against honor. The crime of injury (Article 594 of the penal code) referred to the act of offending someone's honor in their presence and was punishable with up to six months in prison or a fine of up to 516 Euros. The crime of defamation (Article 595, Penal Code) refers to any other situation involving offending one's reputation before many persons, and is punishable with a penalty of up to a year in prison or up to 1032 Euros in fine, doubled to up to two years in prison or a fine of 2065 Euros if the offense consists in the attribution of a determined fact. When the offense happens by the means of the press or by any other means of publicity, or in a public demonstration, the penalty is of imprisonment from six months to three years, or a fine of at least 516 Euros. Both of them were "a querela di parte" crimes, that is, the victim had the right of choosing, in any moment, to stop the criminal prosecution by withdrawing the "querela" (a formal complaint), or even prosecute the fact only with a civil action with no "querela" and therefore no criminal prosecution at all. However, beginning from 15 January 2016, injury is no longer a crime, but a tort, while defamation is still considered a crime like before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Finally, Article 31 of the Penal Code establishes that crimes committed with <a href="/wiki/Malfeasance_in_office" title="Malfeasance in office">abuse of power</a> or with abuse of a profession or <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a>, or with the violation of a duty inherent to that profession or art, lead to the additional penalty of a temporary <a href="/wiki/Ban_(law)" title="Ban (law)">ban</a> in the exercise of that profession or art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>Deliberately false accusations of defamation, as with any other crime, lead to the crime of <a href="/wiki/Calunnia" title="Calunnia">calumny</a> (Article 368, Penal Code), which, under the Italian legal system, is defined as the crime of falsely accusing, before the authorities, one of a crime it didn't commit.</p>
<p>As to the trial, judgment on the legality of the evidence fades into its relevance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Netherlands">Netherlands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Netherlands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the Netherlands, defamation is mostly dealt with by lodging a civil complaint at the District Court. Article 167 of book 6 of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_Code" title="Civil Code">Civil Code</a> holds: "When someone is liable towards another person under this Section because of an incorrect or, by its incompleteness, misleading publication of information of factual nature, the court may, upon a right of action (legal claim) of this other person, order the tortfeasor to publish a correction in a way to be set by court." If the court grants an injunction, the defendant is usually ordered to delete the publication or to publish a rectification statement.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Norway">Norway</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Norway">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Norway, defamation is a crime punished with imprisonment of up to 6 months or a fine (Penal Code, Chapter 23, § 246). When the offense is likely to harm one's "good name" and reputation, or exposes him to hatred, contempt or loss of confidence, the maximum prison term goes up to one year, and if the defamation happens in print, in broadcasting or through an especially aggravating circumstance, imprisonment may reach two years (§ 247). When the offender acts "against his better judgment", he is liable to a maximum prison term of three years (§ 248). According to § 251, defamation lawsuits must be initiated by the offended person, unless the defamatory act was directed to an indefinite group or a large number of persons, when it may also be prosecuted by public authorities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>Under the new Penal Code, decided upon by the Parliament in 2005, defamation will cease to exist as a crime. Rather, any person who believes he or she has been subject to defamation will have to press civil lawsuits. The Penal Code has not taken effect as of 2010, and there are no set date for this.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Poland">Poland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Poland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Poland, defamation is a crime that consists of accusing someone of a <a href="/wiki/Misconduct" title="Misconduct">conduct that may degrade</a> him in <a href="/wiki/Public_opinion" title="Public opinion">public opinion</a> or expose him "to the loss of confidence necessary for a given position, occupation or type of activity". Penalties include fine, limitation of liberty and imprisonment for up to a year (Article 212.1 of the Criminal Code). The penalty is more severe when the offense happens through the <a href="/wiki/Mass_media" title="Mass media">media</a> (Article 212.2).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> When the insult is public and aims at offending a group of people or an individual because of his or their <a href="/wiki/Nationality" title="Nationality">nationality</a>, ethnicity, race, religion or lack of religion, the maximum prison term is 3 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Portugal">Portugal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Portugal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Portugal, defamation crimes are: "defamation" (article 180 of the Penal Code; up to six months in prison, or a fine of up to 240 days), "injuries" (art. 181; up to 3 months in prison, or a fine up to 120 days), and "offense to the memory of a deceased person" (art. 185; up to 6 months in prison or a fine of up 240 days). Penalties are aggravated in cases with publicity (art. 183; up to two years in prison or at least 120 days of fine) and when the victim is an authority (art.184; all other penalties aggravated by an extra half). There is yet the extra penalty of "public knowledge of the court decision" (costs paid by the defamer) (art. 189 of Penal Code) and also the crime of "incitation of a crime" (article 297; up to 3 years in prison, or fine).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spain">Spain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Spain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
