<p>In Spain, the crime of calumny (Article 205 of the Penal Code) consists of offending one's reputation knowing the falsity of the offense, or with a reckless <a href="/wiki/Contempt" title="Contempt">contempt</a> for truth. Penalties for cases with publicity are imprisonment from six months to two years or a fine of 12 to 24 months-fine, and for other cases only a fine of 6 to 12 months-fine (Article 206). Additionally, the crime of injury (Article 208 of the Penal Code) consists of hurting someone's <a href="/wiki/Dignity" title="Dignity">dignity</a>, depreciating his reputation or injuring his <a href="/wiki/Self-esteem" title="Self-esteem">self-esteem</a>, and is only applicable if the offense, by its nature, effects and circumstances, is considered by the general public as strong. Injury has a penalty of fine from 3 to 7 months-fine, or from 6 to 14 months-fine when it's strong and with publicity. According to Article 216, an additional penalty to calumny or injury may be imposed by the judge, determining the publication of the judicial decision (in a newspaper) at the expenses of the defamer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sweden">Sweden</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Sweden">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Sweden, the criminal offense of denigration (<i>ärekränkning</i>) is regulated in Chapter 5 of the Criminal Code. Article 1 regulates defamation (<i>förtal</i>) and consists of pointing out someone as a criminal or as "having a reprehensible way of living", or of providing information about him "intended to cause exposure to the disrespect of others". The penalty is a fine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swedenlaw_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-swedenlaw-88">[88]</a></sup> It is generally not a requirement that the statements are untrue, it is enough if they statements are meant to be vilifying.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jobbet_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-jobbet-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>Article 2 regulates <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aggravation_(legal_concept)" title="Aggravation (legal concept)">gross</a> defamation (<i>grovt förtal</i>) and has a penalty of up to 2 years in prison or a fine. In judging if the crime is gross, the court should consider whether the information, because of its content or the scope of its dissemination, is calculated to produce "serious damage".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swedenlaw_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-swedenlaw-88">[88]</a></sup> For example, if it can be established that the defendant knowingly conveyed untruths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jobbet_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-jobbet-89">[89]</a></sup> Article 4 makes it a crime to defame a deceased person according to Article 1 or 2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swedenlaw_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-swedenlaw-88">[88]</a></sup> Most obviously, the paragraph is meant to make it illegal to defame someones parents as a way to bypass the law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jobbet_89-2"><a href="#cite_note-jobbet-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Article 3 regulates other insulting behavior (<i>förolämpning</i>), not characterized under Article 1 or 2 and is punishable with a fine or, if it's gross, with up to six months of prison or a fine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swedenlaw_88-3"><a href="#cite_note-swedenlaw-88">[88]</a></sup> While an act of defamation involves a third person, it is not a requirement for insulting behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jobbet_89-3"><a href="#cite_note-jobbet-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Under exemptions in the Freedom of the Press Act, Chapter 7, both criminal and civil lawsuits may be brought to court under the laws on denigration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Switzerland">Switzerland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Switzerland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Switzerland, the crime of wilful defamation is punished with a maximum term of three years in prison, or with a fine of at least 30 days-fine, according to Article 174-2 of the Swiss Criminal Code. There is wilful defamation when the offender knows the falsity of his/her allegations and intentionally looks to ruin the <a href="/wiki/Reputation" title="Reputation">reputation</a> of one's victim (see Articles 174-1 and 174-2).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the other hand, defamation is punished only with a maximum monetary penalty of 180 daily penalty units (Article 173-1).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> When it comes to a deceased or absent person, there is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Period_of_prescription" title="Period of prescription">limitation</a> to enforce the law up to 30 years (after the death).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>With the rise of the internet, and also intranets (closed computer networks), defamatory statements may be communicated on webpages or internal memos, without reaching the attention of the courts. Such "closet defamation" may be used to conceal other criminal or negligent acts.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><span id="Defamation_law_in_the_United_Kingdom"></span></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="England_and_Wales">England and Wales</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: England and Wales">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_defamation_law" title="English defamation law">English defamation law</a></div>
<p>Modern libel and slander laws (as implemented in many but not all <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a> nations) in the United Kingdom, and in the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a> are originally descended from English defamation law. The history of defamation law in England is somewhat obscure. Civil actions for damages seem to have been relatively frequent so far back as the reign of <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">Edward I</a> (1272–1307),<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> though it is unknown whether any generally applicable criminal process was in use. The first fully reported case in which libel is affirmed generally to be punishable at common law was tried during the reign of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_I_of_England" title="James I of England">James I</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> From that time, both the criminal and civil remedies have been in full operation.</p>
<p>English law allows actions for libel to be brought in the High Court for any published statements alleged to defame a named or identifiable individual or individuals (under English law companies are legal persons, and allowed to bring suit for defamation<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-indiana1_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-indiana1-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google1_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-google1-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lexology1_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-lexology1-98">[98]</a></sup>) in a manner that causes them loss in their trade or profession, or causes a reasonable person to think worse of them. Allowable defences are justification (the truth of the statement), fair comment (whether the statement was a view that a reasonable person could have held), absolute privilege (whether the statements were made in Parliament or in court, or whether they were fair reports of allegations in the public interest) and qualified privilege (where it is thought that the freedom of expression outweighs the protection of reputation, but not to the degree of granting absolute immunity).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> An offer of amends is a barrier to litigation. A defamatory statement is presumed to be false unless the defendant can prove its truth. Furthermore, to collect compensatory damages, a public official or public figure must prove actual malice (knowing falsity or reckless disregard for the truth).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> A private individual must only prove negligence (not using due care) to collect compensatory damages.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> To collect punitive damages, all individuals must prove actual malice.</p>
<p>Criminal libel was abolished on 12 January 2010 by section 73 of the <a href="/wiki/Coroners_and_Justice_Act_2009" title="Coroners and Justice Act 2009">Coroners and Justice Act 2009</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> There were only a few instances of the criminal libel law being applied. Notably, the Italian anarchist <a href="/wiki/Errico_Malatesta" title="Errico Malatesta">Errico Malatesta</a> was convicted of criminal libel for denouncing the Italian state agent Ennio Belelli in 1912.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Scotland">Scotland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Scotland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Scots_law" title="Scots law">Scots law</a>, as in other jurisdictions that base themselves on the <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law</a> tradition, there is no distinction between libel and slander, and all cases are simply defamation. The equivalent of the defence of justification is "veritas".</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_America">South America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: South America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Argentina">Argentina</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Argentina">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, the crimes of calumny and injury are foreseen in the chapter "Crimes Against Honor" (Articles 109 to 117-bis) of the Penal Code. Calumny is defined as "the false imputation to a determined person of a concrete crime that leads to a lawsuit" (Article 109). However, expressions referring to subjects of public interest or that are not <a href="/wiki/Logical_assertion" title="Logical assertion">assertive</a> don't constitute calumny. Penalty is a fine from 3,000 to 30,000 <a href="/wiki/Argentine_peso" title="Argentine peso">pesos</a>. He who intentionally dishonor or discredit a determined person is punished with a penalty from 1,500 to 20,000 pesos (Article 110).</p>
<p>He who publishes or reproduces, by any means, calumnies and injuries made by others, will be punished as responsible himself for the calumnies and injuries whenever its content is not correctly attributed to the corresponding source. Exceptions are expressions referring to subjects of public interest or that are not assertive (see Article 113). When calumny or injury are committed through the press, a possible extra penalty is the publication of the judicial decision at the expenses of the guilty (Article 114). He who passes to someone else information about a person that is included in a personal database and that one knows to be false, is punished with six months to 3 years in prison. When there is harm to somebody, penalties are aggravated by an extra half (Article 117 bis, §§ 2nd and 3rd).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Brazil">Brazil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Brazil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, defamation is a crime, which is prosecuted either as "defamation" (three months to a year in prison, plus fine; Article 139 of the Penal Code), "calumny" (six months to two years in prison, plus fine; Article 138 of the PC) and/or "injury" (one to six months in prison, or fine; Article 140), with aggravating penalties when the crime is practiced in public (Article 141, item III) or against a state employee because of his regular duties. Incitation to hatred and violence is also foreseen in the Penal Code (incitation to a crime, Article 286). Moreover, in situations like <a href="/wiki/Bullying" title="Bullying">bullying</a> or moral constraint, defamation acts are also covered by the crimes of "illegal constraint" (Article 146 of the Penal Code) and "arbitrary exercise of discretion" (Article 345 of PC), defined as breaking the law as a <a href="/wiki/Vigilante" title="Vigilante">vigilante</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Chile">Chile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Chile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, the crimes of calumny and slanderous allegation <i>(injurias)</i> are covered by Articles 412 to 431 of the Penal Code. Calumny is defined as "the false imputation of a determined crime and that can lead to a public prosecution" (Article 412). If the calumny is written and with publicity, penalty is "lower imprisonment" in its medium degree plus a fine of 11 to 20 "vital wages" when it refers to a crime, or "lower imprisonment" in its minimum degree plus a fine of 6 to 10 "vital wages" when it refers to a <a href="/wiki/Misdemeanor" title="Misdemeanor">misdemeanor</a> (Article 413). If it's not written or with publicity, penalty is "lower imprisonment" in its minimum degree plus a fine of 6 to 15 "vital wages" when it's about a crime, or plus a fine of 6 to 10 "vital wages" when it's about a misdemeanor (Article 414).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Article 25 of the Penal Code, "lower imprisonment" is defined as a prison term between 61 days and five years. According to Article 30, the penalty of "lower imprisonment" in its medium or minimum degrees carries with it also the suspension of the exercise of a public position during the prison term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>Article 416 defines <i>injuria</i> as "all expression said or action performed that dishonors, discredits or causes contempt". Article 417 defines broadly "<i>injurias graves</i>" (grave slander), including the imputation of a crime or misdemeanor that cannot lead to public prosecution, and the imputation of a vice or lack of morality, which are capable of harming considerably the reputation, credit or interests of the offended person. "Grave slander" in written form or with publicity are punished with "lower imprisonment" in its minimum to medium degrees plus a fine of 11 to 20 "vital wages". Calumny or slander of a deceased person (Article 424) can be prosecuted by the spouse, children, grandchildren, parents, grandparents, siblings and <a href="/wiki/Inheritance" title="Inheritance">heirs</a> of the offended person. Finally, according to Article 425, in the case of calumnies and slander published in foreign newspapers, are considered liable all those who from Chilean territory sent articles or gave orders for publication abroad, or contributed to the introduction of such newspapers in Chile with the intention of propagating the calumny and slander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Venezuela">Venezuela</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Venezuela">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In March 2016 a civil action for defamation led to imposition of a four-year prison sentence on a newspaper publisher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSJ031216_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ031216-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Canadian_defamation_law" title="Canadian defamation law">Canadian defamation law</a></div>
<p>As is the case for most <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a> jurisdictions, Canada follows English law on defamation issues (except in <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a> where the private law is derived from French civil law). In common law, defamation covers any communication that tends to lower the esteem of the subject in the minds of ordinary members of the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Probably true statements are not excluded, nor are political opinions. Intent is always presumed, and it is not necessary to prove that the defendant intended to defame. In <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hill_v._Church_of_Scientology_of_Toronto" title="Hill v. Church of Scientology of Toronto">Hill v. Church of Scientology of Toronto</a></i> (1995), the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Canada" title="Supreme Court of Canada">Supreme Court of Canada</a> rejected the <i>actual malice</i> test adopted in the US case <i>New York Times Co. v. Sullivan</i>. Once a claim has been made, the defendant may avail themselves of a defense of justification (the truth), fair comment, responsible communication,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> or privilege. Publishers of defamatory comments may also use the defense of innocent dissemination where they had no knowledge of the nature of the statement, it was not brought to their attention, and they were not negligent. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Quebec, defamation was originally grounded in the law inherited from France. To establish civil liability for defamation, the plaintiff must establish, on a balance of probabilities, the existence of an injury (fault), a wrongful act (damage), and of a causal connection (link of causality) between the two. A person who has made defamatory remarks will not necessarily be civilly liable for them. The plaintiff must further demonstrate that the person who made the remarks committed a wrongful act. Defamation in Quebec is governed by a reasonableness standard, as opposed to strict liability; a defendant who made a false statement would not be held liable if it was reasonable to believe the statement was true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>Regarding defamation on the internet, in 2011 the Supreme Court of Canada held that a person who posts hyperlinks on a website which lead to another site with defamatory content is not publishing that defamatory material for the purposes of libel and defamation law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Criminal_defamation_2">Criminal defamation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=60" title="Edit section: Criminal defamation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>In Canada, the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Blasphemous_libel" title="Blasphemous libel">blasphemous libel</a>" is a crime punished with a maximum term of two years in prison, according to Article 296-1 of the Canadian Criminal Code, as well as the crime of "<a href="/wiki/Defamatory_libel" title="Defamatory libel">defamatory libel</a>" (Article 298), which receives the same penalty (see Article 301). In the specific case of a "libel known to be false" (Article 300), the prison term increases to a maximum of five years. According to Article 298, a defamatory libel "is matter published, without lawful justification or excuse, that is likely to injure the <a href="/wiki/Reputation" title="Reputation">reputation</a> of any person by exposing him to hatred, contempt or ridicule, or that is designed to insult the person of or concerning whom it is published".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>The criminal portion of the law has been rarely applied. In the most recent case, in 1994 Bradley Waugh and Ravin Gill were charged with criminal libel for publicly accusing six prison guards of the racially motivated murder of a black inmate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to an <a href="/wiki/Organization_for_Security_and_Co-operation_in_Europe" title="Organization for Security and Co-operation in Europe">Organization for Security and Co-operation in Europe</a> official report on defamation laws issued in 2005, 57 persons in Canada were accused of defamation, libel and insult, among which 23 were convicted – 9 to prison sentences, 19 to <a href="/wiki/Probation" title="Probation">probation</a> and one to a fine. The average period in prison was 270 days, and the maximum sentence was 1460 days of imprisonment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=61" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_defamation_law" title="United States defamation law">United States defamation law</a></div>
<p>The origins of US defamation law pre-date the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>; one famous 1734 case involving <a href="/wiki/John_Peter_Zenger" title="John Peter Zenger">John Peter Zenger</a> sowed the seed for the later establishment of truth as an absolute defense against libel charges. The outcome of the case is one of <a href="/wiki/Jury_nullification" title="Jury nullification">jury nullification</a>, and not a case where the defense acquitted itself as a matter of law. (Previous English defamation law had not provided the defense of truth.) Though the First Amendment of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Constitution" title="U.S. Constitution">U.S. Constitution</a> was designed to protect freedom of the press, for most of the history of the United States, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Supreme_Court" title="U.S. Supreme Court">Supreme Court</a> neglected to use it to rule on libel cases. This left libel laws, based upon the traditional common law of defamation inherited from the English legal system, mixed across the states. The 1964 case <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Times_Co._v._Sullivan" title="New York Times Co. v. Sullivan">New York Times Co. v. Sullivan</a></i>, however, dramatically altered the nature of libel law in the United States by elevating the fault element for public officials to actual malice—that is, public figures could win a libel suit only if they could demonstrate the publisher's "knowledge that the information was false" or that the information was published "with reckless disregard of whether it was false or not". Later Supreme Court cases dismissed the claim for libel and forbade libel claims for statements that are so ridiculous to be clearly not true, or that involve opinionated subjects such as one's physical state of being.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title='"physical state of being" - physical state of being sounds very testable, "opinionated" is probably the wrong word (June 2015)'>clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Recent<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (June 2015)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> cases<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (June 2015)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> have addressed defamation law and the Internet.</p>
<p>Defamation law in the United States is much less plaintiff-friendly than its counterparts in European and the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth countries</a>. In the United States, a comprehensive discussion of what is and is not libel or slander is difficult, because the definition differs between different states, and under federal law. Some states codify what constitutes slander and libel together into the same set of laws. Criminal libel is rare or nonexistent, depending on the state. Defenses to libel that can result in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Summary_judgement" title="Summary judgement">dismissal before trial</a> include the statement being one of <a href="/wiki/Opinion_privilege" title="Opinion privilege">opinion</a> rather than fact or being "<a href="/wiki/Fair_comment" title="Fair comment">fair comment</a> and criticism". Truth is always a defense.</p>
<p>However, American writers and publishers are protected<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (June 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> from foreign libel judgments not compliant with the <a href="/wiki/First_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="First Amendment to the United States Constitution">US First Amendment</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Libel_tourism" title="Libel tourism">libel tourism</a>, by the <a href="/wiki/SPEECH_Act" title="SPEECH Act">SPEECH Act</a>, which was passed by the <a href="/wiki/111th_United_States_Congress" title="111th United States Congress">111th United States Congress</a> and signed into law by President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> in 2010. It is based on the New York State 2008 Libel Terrorism Protection Act (also known as "Rachel’s Law," after <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Ehrenfeld" title="Rachel Ehrenfeld">Rachel Ehrenfeld</a> who initiated the state and federal laws). Both the New York state law and the federal law were passed unanimously.</p>
<p>Most states recognize that some categories of statements are considered to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Defamation_per_se" title="Defamation per se">defamatory <i>per se</i></a>, such that people making a defamation claim for these statements do not need to prove that the statement was defamatory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>Supreme Court Stances on Group Defamation</b></p>
<p>Group libel has been on many occasions shown to be found by United States courts to be a crime which was punishable under common law. There were three notable early cases in United States law which found group libel to be a criminal offense. The first of these cases was State v. Brady (1890) The holding of this court found that “The law is elementary that a libel need not be on a particular person, but may be upon a family, or a class of persons, if the tendency of the publication is to stir up riot and disorder, and incite to a breach of the peace.” <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> This holding is similar to that of King v. Osborne, in that the court found the prevention of riots to take priority over the protection of speech.</p>
<p>Jones v. State of Texas (1897) took place a few years after Brady and held a similar view on group libel. This case was, however, different in that it concerned the defamation of streetcar conductors in Galveston. The court still sided with the state, saying that “It therefore would be a violation of our statute to libel any sect, company, or class of men without naming any person in particular who may belong to said class”.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Going further than strictly outlawing libel against a religious or racial group, the Jones court found that libel against any group, even a class of workers, had potential to lead to violence between groups.</p>
<p>People v. Spielman (1925) upheld same statute as the one in Beauharnais. In this case, publications defamed members of the Knights of Columbus, falsely assigning an oath to them. In this case the defendant was found guilty of a libel on both “the membership of the American Legion and certain named members of that organization.” <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-20">[20]</a></sup> The holding that individual members were libeled relied on doctrine from the Osborne case. Though these individual members were not named in the publication, their ties to the legion gave them adequate claim to a criminal libel offense. These three cases played a large role in solidifying the American conception of group libel law as it was interpreted in the Beauharnais case.</p>
<p>Though the common law interpretation of group libel law has generally been referred to in United States court cases prior to the case of Beauharnais v. Illinois, the courts have not always taken this stance. There are two notable group libel cases prior to Beauharnais where the court went contrary to the holding of Osborne. This first of these cases was Drozda v. State (1920). This case examined an instance of libel on the leaders of Bohemian national organization. The court dismissed their claim, stating that “A government or other body politic, a corporation, religious system, race of people, or a political party, are not subject to criminal libel. Nor could a publication referring generally to any of these be made specific or libelous.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> This judge believed that since the libel in question was directed towards "those people whom you call leaders", there was not sufficient evidence that those claiming to have been libeled against actually had any comments directed towards them.</p>
<p>The court in People v. Edmonson (1930) also denied claims to an apparent case of group libel. In this case, the defendant was accused of libel towards the Jewish community. The judge sided with the defendant, writing that “...such an indictment cannot be sustained under the laws of this State, and that no such indictment as one based upon defamatory matter directed against a group or community so large as "all persons of the Jewish Religion" has ever been sustained in this or any other jurisdiction.” The judge further said that “when one realizes how many forms of religion might consider themselves libeled and seek legal redress, where our laws so extended, and when we reflect on how our courts might, in such event, find themselves forced into the position of arbiters of religious truth, it is apparent that more would be lost than could be gained by attempting to protect the good name of a religion by an appeal to the criminal law”.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> In this case, the judge finds that it would be unreasonable to expect courts to take on the responsibility of deciding whether statements towards a religion should or should or should not be considered libel. Though group libel generally favored the Osborne holding prior to the Beauharnais case, there is also a well documented record of United States courts taking a position which more closely resembles that of the Orme and Nutt holding.</p>
<p><b>Beauharnais v. Illinois</b></p>
<p>Beauharnais v. Illinois is one of the better known cases of group libel in the United States judicial system. Joseph Beauharnais was arrested in 1950 for distributing leaflets in Chicago. Within these leaflets, Beauharnais called upon the Chicago government to take action to address “the constant and continuous invasion, harassment and encroachment by the Negro” An Illinois law outlawed the distribution of any material which “portrays depravity, criminality, unchastity, or lack of virtue of a class of citizens, of any race, color, creed or religion which said publication or exhibition exposes the citizens of any race, color, creed or religion to contempt, derision, or obloquy or which is productive of breach of the peace or riots"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-123">[123]</a></sup> Beauharnais disagreed with this law, and believed that his publications should be viewed as protected speech rather than group libel.</p>
<p>In a 5-4 decision, the court found Beauharnais guilty of libel. In his majority opinion, Justice Frankfurter wrote that Beauharnais’ comments provoked hostility, and, given Illinois’ history of racial tensions, should be outlawed.</p>
<p>Justice Black, in his dissent, stated that he believed that the statute could be abused to protect speech that otherwise should not be protected. However Frankfurt disagreed and said that "Every power may be abused, but the possibility of abuse is a poor reason for denying Illinois the power to adopt measures against criminal libels sanctioned by centuries of Anglo-American law."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-123">[123]</a></sup> Group libel laws, according to Frankfurt, played an important role in the history of common law, and its existence prevents speech that could lead to violence from being recognized as protected speech.</p>
<p>Though the Beauharnais case seemed to set a strong precedent protecting criminal group libel laws at the time, subsequent cases took a stance which more strongly favors speech protections. R. A. V. v. City of St. Paul (1992) is one of the most notable of these cases. In St. Paul, Minnesota, it was a crime to place something in public which could cause "anger, alarm, or resentment …on the basis of race, color, creed, religion, or gender." Representing the unanimous court that held the ordinance invalid on its face, Justice Scalia explained and qualified the categorical exclusions for defamation, obscenity, and fighting words. These categories of speech are not “entirely invisible to the Constitution,” but instead “can, consistently with the First Amendment, be regulated because of their constitutionally proscribable content.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> In this case, Scalia believed that the St. Paul law was a clear case of viewpoint based discrimination, and therefore unconstitutional.</p>
<p>The Court in Virginia v. Black (2003) held in a 7-2 decision that its opinion in R.A.V. did not make it unconstitutional for a state to prohibit burning a cross with the intent of intimidating any person or group of persons because it prevents intimidation rather than discriminate on the basis of a defendant’s beliefs. In her opinion, Justice O'Connor wrote that “as a factual matter it is not true that cross burners direct their intimidating conduct solely to racial or religious minorities. . .. The First Amendment permits Virginia to outlaw cross burning done with the intent to intimidate because burning a cross is a particularly virulent form of intimidation. Instead of prohibiting all intimidating messages, Virginia may choose to regulate this subset of intimidating messages…” <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> She clarified that "a State may choose to prohibit only those forms of intimidation that are most likely to inspire fear of bodily harm."</p>
<p>Justice Thomas dissented to this holding, giving similar to arguments made for prohibiting flag burning. He wrote that all cross burning should be exempt from the 1st amendment“due to the historical association of cross-burning with terrorism”</p>
<p>Justice Souter had their own opinion, defending all cross burning, even those acts which are committed to cause fear because of R.A.V., citing a problem with "the statute’s content-based distinction.”</p>
<p>While common law has traditionally interpreted group libel laws in a way which protects against defamation, subsequent United States court holdings such as that in R. A. V. v. City of St. Paul (1992) and Virginia v. Black (2003) have taken a stance that is more protective of free speech.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Record_awards">Record awards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=62" title="Edit section: Record awards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The record libel verdict in the United States was rendered in 1997 against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dow_Jones" title="Dow Jones">Dow Jones</a> in favor of MMAR Group Inc., awarding $222.7 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> However, the verdict was dismissed in 1999 amid allegations that MMAR failed to disclose audiotapes made by its employees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>The record verdict rendered in favour of an individual was the award of $35.5 million against the Russian newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/Izvestia" title="Izvestia">Izvestia</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> in favor of entrepreneur <a href="/wiki/Alex_Konanykhin" title="Alex Konanykhin">Alex Konanykhin</a>, who also won a $3 million judgment against <i><a href="/wiki/Kommersant" title="Kommersant">Kommersant</a></i>, another Russian newspaper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Defamation_per_se">Defamation <i>per se</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=63" title="Edit section: Defamation per se">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The four (4) categories of slander that are actionable <i><a href="/wiki/Illegal_per_se" title="Illegal per se">per se</a></i> are (i) accusing someone of a crime; (ii) alleging that someone has a foul or loathsome disease; (iii) adversely reflecting on a person's fitness to conduct their business or trade; and (iv) imputing serious sexual misconduct. Here again, the plaintiff need only prove that someone had made the statement to any third party. No proof of special damages is required. It should be noted that truth is an absolute defense to defamation per se. This means that even if the statement would be considered defamatory per se if false, if the defendant establishes that it's in fact true, an action for defamation per se cannot survive. In May 2012 an appeals court in New York, citing changes in public policy with regard to <a href="/wiki/Homosexuality" title="Homosexuality">homosexuality</a>, ruled that describing someone as <a href="/wiki/Gay" title="Gay">gay</a> is not defamation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYGayRuling_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYGayRuling-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Criminal_defamation_3">Criminal defamation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=64" title="Edit section: Criminal defamation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>At the federal level, there are no criminal defamation or insult laws in the United States. However, on the state level, 17 states and 2 territories have criminal defamation laws on the books: <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kansas" title="Kansas">Kansas</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire" title="New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Islands" title="Virgin Islands">Virgin Islands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> Two addition states had such laws in 2005;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a>'s law<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> was repealed in 2012<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, and <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a>'s was repealed in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mexico">Mexico</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=65" title="Edit section: Mexico">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Crimes of calumny, defamation and slanderous allegation <i>(injurias)</i> have been abolished in the Federal Penal Code as well as in 15 states. These crimes remain in the penal codes of 17 states, where penalty is, in average, from 1.1 years (for ones convicted for slanderous allegation) to 3.8 years in jail (for those convicted for calumny).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceania">Oceania</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=66" title="Edit section: Oceania">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=67" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Law_in_Australia" title="Law in Australia">Australian law</a> tends to follow English law on defamation issues, although there are differences introduced by statute and by the implied constitutional limitation on governmental powers to limit speech of a political nature established in <i><a href="/wiki/Lange_v_Australian_Broadcasting_Corporation" title="Lange v Australian Broadcasting Corporation">Lange v Australian Broadcasting Association (1997)</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> It is interesting to note that in Common Law, not all statements that are injurious to a person are necessarily defamatory. The statement must be about that particular person for it to be categorised as defamatory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2005, uniform defamation laws were introduced across Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> The laws made a number of changes to the Common Law, including:</p>
<ul>
<li>Abolishing the distinction between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Libel" title="Libel">libel</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slander" title="Slander">slander</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup></li>
<li>Providing new defences including that of triviality, where it is a defence to the publication of a defamatory matter if the defendant proves that the circumstances of publication were such that the plaintiff was unlikely to sustain any harm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-158">[158]</a></sup></li>
<li>The defences against defamation may be negated if there is proof the publication was actuated by malice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-158">[158]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>A recent judgment of the <a href="/wiki/High_Court_of_Australia" title="High Court of Australia">High Court of Australia</a> has significant consequences on interpretation of the law. On 10 December 2002, the High Court of Australia handed down its judgment in the Internet defamation dispute in the case of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dow_Jones_v_Gutnick" title="Dow Jones v Gutnick">Dow Jones v Gutnick</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> The judgment established that Internet-published foreign publications that defamed an Australian in their Australian reputation could be held accountable under Australian defamation law. The case gained worldwide attention and is often said, inaccurately, to be the first of its kind. A similar case that predates <i>Dow Jones v Gutnick</i> is <i><a href="/wiki/Berezovsky_v_Michaels" title="Berezovsky v Michaels">Berezovsky v Forbes</a></i> in England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>Among the various common law jurisdictions, some Americans have presented a visceral and vocal reaction to the <i>Gutnick</i> decision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> On the other hand, the decision mirrors similar decisions in many other jurisdictions such as England, Scotland, France, Canada and Italy.</p>
<p>Uniform Defamation Laws was passed in Australia in January 2006 severely restricting the right of corporations to sue for defamation (see, e.g., <i>Defamation Act 2005</i> (Vic), s 9). This makes the defamation laws across states and territories similar. The only corporations excluded from the general ban are those not for profit<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> or those with less than 10 employees and not affiliated with another company. Corporations may, however, still sue for the tort of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Injurious_falsehood" title="Injurious falsehood">injurious falsehood</a>, where the burden of proof is greater than for mere defamation, because the plaintiff must show that the defamation was made with malice and resulted in economic loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 2006 reforms also established across all Australian states the availability of truth as an unqualified defense; previously a number of states only allowed a defense of truth with the condition that a public benefit existed. The defendant needs to prove that the defamatory imputations are substantially true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> Lawyers for plaintiffs often use the initial high expense of lodging a defense to their advantage, demanding the immediate payment of tens of thousands of dollars from potential defendants. Even though a plaintiff may have little chance of winning deformation action, or even having the courts accept the case, the unpredictable outcomes of such cases usually sees defence lawyers asking for large payments before any legal work begins, and advising clients to pay the out-of-court demands.</p>
<p>The law as it currently stands in Australia was summarized in the 2015 case of Duffy v Google by Justice Blue in the Supreme Court of South Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The tort can be divided up into the following ingredients:</p>
<ul>
<li>the defendant participates in publication to a third party of a body of work;</li>
<li>the body of work contains a passage alleged to be defamatory;</li>
<li>the passage conveys an imputation;</li>
<li>the imputation is about the plaintiff;</li>
<li>the imputation is damaging to the plaintiff’s reputation.
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_Blue" title="Malcolm Blue">Justice Blue</a>, Duffy v Google Inc, paragraph 158.</cite></div>
</li>
</ul>
</blockquote>
<p>A more recent case in defamation law was <i>Hockey v Fairfax Media Publications Pty Limited</i> [2015], heard in the Federal Court of Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-166">[166]</a></sup> This judgment was significant as it demonstrated that tweets, consisting of even as little as three words, can be defamatory, as was held in this case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-166">[166]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_law">Religious law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=68" title="Edit section: Religious law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Hebrew term <a href="/wiki/Lashon_hara" title="Lashon hara">lashon hara</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">halakhic</a> term for derogatory speech about another person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jewfaq_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-jewfaq-167">[167]</a></sup> <i>Lashon hara</i> differs from defamation in that its focus is on the use of true speech for a wrongful purpose, rather than falsehood and harm arising. By contrast, <i>hotzaat shem ra</i> ("spreading a bad name"), also called <i>hotzaat diba</i>, consists of untrue remarks, and is best translated as "slander" or "defamation". <i>Hotzaat shem ra</i> is worse, and consequently a graver sin, than <i>lashon hara</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jewfaq_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-jewfaq-167">[167]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> theology there are seen to be two sins, that of lying and that of impinging on a person's right to a reputation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> It is considered to be closed to <a href="/wiki/Detraction" title="Detraction">detraction</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sin" title="Sin">sin</a> of revealing previously unknown faults or sins of another person to a third person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Related_torts">Related torts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=69" title="Edit section: Related torts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Some jurisdictions have a separate <a href="/wiki/Tort" title="Tort">tort</a> or delict of "<a href="/wiki/Verbal_injury" title="Verbal injury">verbal injury</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Intentional_infliction_of_emotional_distress" title="Intentional infliction of emotional distress">intentional infliction of emotional distress</a>", "outrageousness", or "convicium", involving the making of a statement, even if truthful, intended to harm the claimant out of malice; some have a separate tort or delict of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Invasion_of_privacy" title="Invasion of privacy">invasion of privacy</a>" in which the making of a true statement may give rise to liability: but neither of these comes under the general heading of "defamation". Some jurisdictions also have the tort of "<a href="/wiki/False_light" title="False light">false light</a>", in which a statement may be technically true, but so misleading as to be defamatory. There is also, in almost all jurisdictions, a tort or delict of "<a href="/wiki/Misrepresentation" title="Misrepresentation">misrepresentation</a>", involving the making of a statement that is untrue even though not defamatory. Thus a surveyor who states a house is free from risk of flooding has not defamed anyone, but may still be liable to someone who purchases the house relying on this statement. Other increasingly common claims similar to defamation in U.S. law are claims that a famous trademark has been diluted through tarnishment, see generally <a href="/wiki/Trademark_dilution" title="Trademark dilution">trademark dilution</a>, "<a href="/wiki/Tortious_interference" title="Tortious interference">intentional interference with contract</a>", and "negligent misrepresentation".</p>
<p>Criminal laws prohibiting protests at funerals, <a href="/wiki/Sedition" title="Sedition">sedition</a>, false statements in connection with elections, and the use of profanity in public, are also often used in contexts similar to criminal libel actions.</p>
<p>The boundaries of a court's power to hold individuals in "contempt of court" for what amounts to alleged defamatory statements about judges or the court process by attorneys or other people involved in court cases is also not well established in many common law countries.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=70" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Defamation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=71" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">LeRoy Miller, Roger (2011). <i>Business Law Today: The Essentials</i>. United States: South-Western Cengage Learning. p. 127. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1-133-19135-5" title="Special:BookSources/1-133-19135-5">1-133-19135-5</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADefamation&amp;rft.aufirst=Roger&amp;rft.aulast=LeRoy+Miller&amp;rft.btitle=Business+Law+Today%3A+The+Essentials&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=1-133-19135-5&amp;rft.pages=127&amp;rft.place=United+States&amp;rft.pub=South-Western+Cengage+Learning&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">False:</span>
<ul>
<li><span class="reference-text">Ron Hankin, <i>Navigating the Legal Minefield of Private Investigations: A Career-Saving Guide for Private Investigators, Detectives, And Security Police</i>, Looseleaf Law Publications, 2008, p. 59. "There are five essential elements to defamation: (1) The accusation is false; and (2) it impeaches the subject's character; and (3) it is published to a third person; and (4) it damages the reputation of the subject; and (5) that the accusation is done intentionally or with fault such as wanton disregard of facts."</span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text">Roger LeRoy Miller, Gaylord A. Jentz, <i>Business Law Today: The Essentials</i>, Cengage Learning, 2007, p. 115. "In other words, making a negative statement about another person is not defamation unless the statement is false and represents something as a fact (for example, 'Vladik cheats on his taxes') rather than a personal opinion (for example, 'Vladik is a jerk')."</span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text">Michael G. Parkinson, L. Marie Parkinson, <i>Law for advertising, broadcasting, journalism, and public relations</i>, Routledge, 2006, p. 273. "Simplifying a very complicated decision, the court said that because the plaintiff must prove a statement is false in order to win an action in defamation, it is impossible to win an action in defamation if the statement, by its very nature, cannot be proven false."</span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text">Edward Lee Lamoureux, Steven L. Baron, Claire Stewart, <i>Intellectual property law and interactive media: free for a fee</i>, Peter Lang, 2009, p. 190. "A statement can only be defamatory if it is false; therefore true statements of fact about others, regardless of the damage rendered, are not defamatory (although such comments might represent other sorts of privacy or hate speech violations). Defamation may occur when one party (the eventual defendant if a case goes forward) writes or says something that is false about a second party (plaintiff) such that some third party "receives" the communication, and the communication of false information damages the plaintiff".</span></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Linda L. Edwards, J. Stanley Edwards, Patricia Kirtley Wells, <i>Tort Law for Legal Assistants</i>, Cengage Learning, 2008, p. 390. "Libel refers to written defamatory statements; slander refers to oral statements. Libel encompasses communications occurring in 'physical form'... defamatory statements on records and computer tapes are considered libel rather than slander."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-martin-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-martin_4-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://netlaw.samford.edu/Martin/AdvancedTorts/falselight.htm" rel="nofollow">False light</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20080227000000/http://netlaw.samford.edu/Martin/AdvancedTorts/falselight.htm" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> February 27, 2008, at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>. by Professor Edward C. <li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Folkard, Henry Coleman (1908). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=7C8WAAAAYAAJ&amp;lpg=PA579&amp;ots=6o0ssMhlBM&amp;dq=libel%20act%201843&amp;pg=PA480#v=onepage&amp;q=public%20benefit&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow"><i>The Law of Slander and Libel</i></a>. <li id="cite_note-RPdefamation-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-RPdefamation_27-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-RPdefamation_27-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Republic of the Philippines (11 November 1980). <a class="external text" href="http://www.chanrobles.com/revisedpenalcodeofthephilippinesbook2.htm" rel="nofollow">"The Revised Penal Code"</a>. Chan Robles law Firm<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2012-02-02</span></span>. <q>Art. 353. Definition of libel. – A libel is public and malicious imputation of a crime, or of a vice or defect, real or imaginary, or any act, omission, condition, status, or circumstance tending to cause the dishonor, discredit, or contempt of a natural or juridical person, or to blacken the memory of one who is dead.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADefamation&amp;rft.au=Republic+of+the+Philippines&amp;rft.btitle=The+Revised+Penal+Code&amp;rft.date=1980-11-11&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.chanrobles.com%2Frevisedpenalcodeofthephilippinesbook2.htm&amp;rft.pub=Chan+Robles+law+Firm&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

The Attorney General of Antigua <a href="/wiki/Tim_Hector" title="Tim Hector">(see Tim Hector)</a> where "public order" laws were used to suppress an opposition newspaper as it were defamatory and therefore likely to stoke disorder, until the law was ruled unconstitutional</li>
